<p>"<b>Aryan</b>" (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɛər/ 'are' in 'bare'">ɛər</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>, <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɛər/ 'are' in 'bare'">ɛər</span><span title="/j/ 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>, <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ær/ 'arr' in 'marry'">ær</span><span title="/-/ affix">-</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a term meaning "noble" which was used as a self-designation by <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indian</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Iranian" title="Iranian">Iranian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranian people</a>. The word was used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_people" title="Indo-Aryan people">Indic people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a> in India as an ethnic label for themselves, as well as to refer to the noble class and geographic location known as <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80ry%C4%81varta" title="Āryāvarta">Āryāvarta</a></i> where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_people" title="Indo-Aryan people">Indo-Aryan</a> culture was based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gopal_1990_70_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gopal_1990_70-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The closely related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_people" title="Iranian people">Iranian people</a> also used the term as an ethnic label for themselves in the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> scriptures, and the word forms the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymological" title="Etymological">etymological</a> source of the country <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aryans_and_British_India_by_Thomas_R._Trautman_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aryans_and_British_India_by_Thomas_R._Trautman-7">[7]</a></sup> It was believed in the 19th century that it was also a self-designation used by all <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-Europeans</a>, a theory that has now been abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup> Scholars point out that, even in ancient times, the idea of being an "Aryan" was religious, cultural and linguistic, not racial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony200711_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony200711-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Drawing on misinterpreted references in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rig_Veda" title="Rig Veda">Rig Veda</a> by Western scholars in the 19th century, the term "Aryan" was adopted as a <a href="/wiki/Historical_race_concepts" title="Historical race concepts">racial category</a> through the work of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a>, whose ideology of race was based on an idea of blonde northern European "Aryans" who had migrated across the world and founded all major civilizations, before being degraded through racial mixture with local populations. Through <a href="/wiki/Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain" title="Houston Stewart Chamberlain">Houston Stewart Chamberlain</a>, Gobineau's ideas later influenced the <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Nazi racial ideology</a>, which also saw "Aryan peoples" as innately superior to other putative racial groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony20079-11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony20079-11-12">[12]</a></sup> The atrocities committed in the name of this racial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aryanism" title="Aryanism">aryanism</a> caused the term to be abandoned by most academics; and, in present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academia</a>, the term "Aryan" has been replaced in most cases by the terms "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranian</a>" and "Aryan" is now mostly limited to its appearance in the term of the "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan languages</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica2-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English word "Aryan" is borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <i>ārya</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%86%E0%A4%B0%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%AF" title="wikt:आर्य">आर्य</a>, meaning "noble" or "noble one".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aryans_and_British_India_by_Thomas_R._Trautman_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aryans_and_British_India_by_Thomas_R._Trautman-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-14">[14]</a></sup> However, as a translation of Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Arianus</a></i> (derived from Old Persian <i>ariya</i>), <i>Arian</i> has "long been in English language use". It was reintroduced into English with the new spelling by <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(philologist)" title="William Jones (philologist)">William Jones</a> in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/220px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/330px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/440px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The earliest epigraphically attested reference to the word <i>arya</i> occurs in the 6th-century B.C. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">Behistun inscription</a>, which describes itself to have been composed "in <i>arya</i> [language or script]" (§ 70). As is also the case for all other Old Iranian language usage, the <i>arya</i> of the inscription does not signify anything but "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Iran" title="Etymology of Iran">Iranian</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gershevitch_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gershevitch-15">[15]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Philologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.P._Mallory" title="J.P. Mallory">J.P. Mallory</a> argues that "As an ethnic designation, the word [Aryan] is most properly limited to the Indo-Iranians, and most justly to the latter where it still gives its name to the country Iran (from the Avestan genitive plural airyanam through later Iranian eran to iran). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanskrit_and_Avestan">Sanskrit and Avestan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sanskrit and Avestan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In early <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedic</a> literature, the term <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80ry%C4%81varta" title="Āryāvarta">Āryāvarta</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: आर्यावर्त, <i>abode of the Aryans</i>) was the name given to northern India, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_people" title="Indo-Aryan people">Indo-Aryan</a> culture was based. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manusm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Manusmṛti">Manusmṛti</a> (2.22) gives the name <i>Āryāvarta</i> to "the tract between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalaya</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vindhya" title="Vindhya">Vindhya</a> ranges, from the Eastern (Bay of Bengal) to the Western Sea (Arabian Sea)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gopal_1990_70_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gopal_1990_70-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially the term was used as a national name to designate those who worshipped the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic</a> deities (especially <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>) and followed Vedic culture (e.g. performance of sacrifice, <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">Yajna</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedic_dictionary_of_Vedic_terms.2C_Volume_1_By_Swami_Parmeshwaranand_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedic_dictionary_of_Vedic_terms.2C_Volume_1_By_Swami_Parmeshwaranand-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Indo-Iranian">Proto-Indo-Iranian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Proto-Indo-Iranian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Sanskrit term comes from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">proto-Indo-Iranian</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-Iranian/arya-" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-Iranian/arya-">*arya-</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[note 1]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laroche_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laroche-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szemer.C3.A9nyi_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szemer.C3.A9nyi-20">[19]</a></sup> or *aryo-,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[note 2]</a></sup> the name used by the Indo-Iranians to designate themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel20001_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel20001-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304-21">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zend" title="Zend">Zend</a> <i>airya</i> 'venerable' and <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> <i>ariya</i> are also derivates of <i>*aryo-</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304-21">[20]</a></sup> and are also self-designations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[note 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>, the original self-identifier lives on in ethnic names like "<a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_dialect" title="Iron dialect">Iron</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R_Schmitt_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-R_Schmitt-29">[24]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Iran" title="Name of Iran">name of Iran</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian word</a> for land/place of the Aryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-30">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Proto-Indo-Iranians">Pre-Proto-Indo-Iranians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pre-Proto-Indo-Iranians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Proto-Indo-Iranian term is hypothesized to have <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">proto-Indo-European</a> origins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laroche_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laroche-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szemer.C3.A9nyi_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Szemer.C3.A9nyi-20">[19]</a></sup> while according to Szemerényi it is probably a Near-Eastern loanword from the Ugaritic <i>ary</i>, kinsmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArbeitman1981930_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArbeitman1981930-31">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been postulated the Proto-Indo-European root word is <i>*haerós</i> with the meanings "members of one's own (ethnic) group, peer, freeman" as well as the Indo-Iranian meaning of Aryan. Derived from it were words like</p>
<ul>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> prefix <i>arā-</i> meaning member of one's own group, peer, companion and friend;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> <i>aire</i> meaning freeman and noble</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> personal names with <i>Ario-</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">Avestan</a> <i>airya-</i> meaning Aryan, Iranian in the larger sense</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Indic_language" title="Old Indic language">Old Indic</a> <i>ari-</i> meaning attached to, faithful, devoted person and kinsman</li>
<li>Old Indic <i>aryá-</i> meaning kind, favourable, attached to and devoted</li>
<li>Old Indic <i>árya-</i> meaning Aryan, faithful to the Vedic religion.</li>
</ul>
<p>The word <i>*haerós</i> itself is believed to have come from the root <i>*haer</i> meaning `put together'. The original meaning in Proto-Indo-European had a clear emphasis on the "in-group status" as distinguished from that of outsiders, particularly those captured and incoporated into the group as slaves. While in Anatolia, the base word has come to emphasize personal relationship, in Indo-Iranian the word has taken a more ethnic meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997213_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997213-32">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>A review of numerous other ideas, and the various problems with each is given by <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Szemer%C3%A9nyi" title="Oswald Szemerényi">Oswald Szemerényi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szemer.C3.A9nyi_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Szemer.C3.A9nyi-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_usage">Scholarly usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scholarly usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-Europeans</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup> during the 19th century, it was proposed that "Aryan" was also the self-designation of the Proto-Indo-Europeans, a hypothesis which has been abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Aryan language family": the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan languages</a> (including the Dardic), <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuristani_languages" title="Nuristani languages">Nuristani languages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdelman1999221_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdelman1999221-33">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan languages</a> specifically, also called Indic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-An_Introduction_to_the_Indo-European_Languages_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-An_Introduction_to_the_Indo-European_Languages-34">[29]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_usage">Contemporary usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Contemporary usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_and_Iranian_nationalism">Indian and Iranian nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Indian and Iranian nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The term "Aryan" is used by <a href="/wiki/Indian_nationalism" title="Indian nationalism">Indian nationalists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_nationalism" title="Iranian nationalism">Iranian nationalists</a> to refer themselves as Aryan in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_migration_theory" title="Indo-Aryan migration theory">Indo-Aryan migration theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (August 2016)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazism_and_White_Supremacy">Nazism and White Supremacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nazism and White Supremacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>During the 19th century it was proposed that "Aryan" was also the self-designation of the Proto-Indo-Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup> Based on speculations that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_Urheimat_hypotheses" title="Proto-Indo-European Urheimat hypotheses">Proto-Indo-European homeland</a> was located in northern Europe, a 19th-century hypothesis which is now abandoned, the word developed a racialist meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup> It has been used in <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Nazi racial theory</a> to describe persons corresponding to the "<a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic</a>" physical ideal of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> (the "<a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>" ideology).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[note 5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_and_adaptation_in_other_languages">Usage and adaptation in other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Usage and adaptation in other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Indian.2FSanskrit_literature">In Indian/Sanskrit literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In Indian/Sanskrit literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and related Indic languages, <i>ārya</i> means "one who does noble deeds; a noble one". <a href="/wiki/%C4%80ry%C4%81varta" title="Āryāvarta">Āryāvarta</a> "abode of the <i>ārya</i>s" is a common name for <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a> in Sanskrit literature. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manusm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Manusmṛti">Manusmṛti</a></i> (2.22) gives the name to "the tract between the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalaya</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vindhya_Range" title="Vindhya Range">Vindhya ranges</a>, from the Eastern Sea to the Western Sea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[34]</a></sup> The title <i>ārya</i> was used with various modifications throughout the Indian Subcontinent. <a href="/wiki/Kharavela" title="Kharavela">Kharavela</a>, the Emperor of Kalinga of around 1 BCE, is referred to as an <i>ārya</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Hathigumpha_inscription" title="Hathigumpha inscription">Hathigumpha inscriptions</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Udayagiri_and_Khandagiri_Caves" title="Udayagiri and Khandagiri Caves">Udayagiri and Khandagiri Caves</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhubaneswar" title="Bhubaneswar">Bhubaneswar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gurjara-Pratihara" title="Gurjara-Pratihara">Gurjara-Pratihara</a> rulers in the tenth century were titled "<a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">Maharajadhiraja</a> of Āryāvarta".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup> Various Indian religions, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, use the term <i>ārya</i> as an epithet of honour; a similar usage is found in the name of <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a>.</p>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, <i>ārya</i> is used as an honorific for many characters including <a href="/wiki/Hanuman" title="Hanuman">Hanuman</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centum_Satem_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="500" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Centum_Satem_map.png/300px-Centum_Satem_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Centum_Satem_map.png/450px-Centum_Satem_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Centum_Satem_map.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centum_Satem_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Indo-European language throughout Europe and the Middle East 500 BCE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Iranian_literature">In Iranian literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In Iranian literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike the several meanings connected with <i>ārya-</i> in <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages#Old_Indo-Aryan" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Old Indo-Aryan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> term only has an ethnic meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup> That is in contrast to Indian usage, in which several secondary meanings evolved, the meaning of <i>ar-</i> as a self-identifier is preserved in Iranian usage, hence <a href="/wiki/Iran_(word)" title="Iran (word)">the word "Iran"</a>. The <i>airya</i> meant "Iranian", and Iranian <i><a href="/wiki/Aniran" title="Aniran">anairya</a></i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup> meant and means "non-Iranian". <i>Arya</i> may also be found as an ethnonym in Iranian languages, e.g., Alan and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> <i>Iran</i> and <a href="/wiki/Ossetian_language" title="Ossetian language">Ossetian</a> <i>Ir/Iron</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup> The name is itself equivalent to Aryan, where Iran means "land of the Aryans,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey2-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Language.2C_Gherardo_Gnoli_2002_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Language.2C_Gherardo_Gnoli_2002-46">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalby.2C_Andrew_2004_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby.2C_Andrew_2004-48">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup> and has been in use since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> times<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalby.2C_Andrew_2004_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby.2C_Andrew_2004-48">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> clearly uses airya/airyan as an ethnic name (Vd. 1; Yt. 13.143-44, etc.), where it appears in expressions such as airyāfi; daiŋˊhāvō "Iranian lands, peoples", airyō.šayanəm "land inhabited by Iranians", and airyanəm vaējō vaŋhuyāfi; dāityayāfi; "Iranian stretch of the good Dāityā", the river Oxus, the modern Āmū Daryā.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> sources also use this term for <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> which is a testament to the antiquity of the Persian language and which is related to most of the languages/dialects spoken in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> including modern <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gilaki_language" title="Gilaki language">Gilaki</a> makes it clear that Iranians referred to themselves as Arya.</p>
<p>The term "Airya/Airyan" appears in the royal Old Persian inscriptions in three different contexts:</p>
<ol>
<li>As the name of the language of the Old Persian version of the inscription of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun" title="Behistun">Behistun</a></li>
<li>As the ethnic background of Darius I in inscriptions at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naqsh-e-Rostam" title="Naqsh-e-Rostam">Naqsh-e-Rostam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> (Dna, Dse) and <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> in the inscription from <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> (Xph)</li>
<li>As the definition of the God of the Aryans, <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazdā</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Elamite_language" title="Elamite language">Elamite language</a> version of the Behistun inscription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>For example in the Dna and Dse Darius and Xerxes describe themselves as "An Achaemenian, A Persian son of a Persian and an Aryan, of Aryan stock".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-50">[44]</a></sup> Although Darius the Great called his language the Aryan language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-50">[44]</a></sup> modern scholars refer to it as <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-50">[44]</a></sup> because it is the ancestor of modern <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Old Persian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avestan_language" title="Avestan language">Avestan</a> evidence is confirmed by the Greek sources".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup> Herodotus in his Histories remarks about the Iranian Medes that: "These Medes were called anciently by all people Arians; " (7.62).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup> In Armenian sources, the Parthians, Medes and Persians are collectively referred to as Aryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[46]</a></sup> Eudemus of Rhodes apud Damascius (Dubitationes et solutiones in Platonis Parmenidem 125 bis) refers to "the Magi and all those of Iranian (áreion) lineage"; Diodorus Siculus (1.94.2) considers <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> (Zathraustēs) as one of the Arianoi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-6"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, in his <i>Geography</i>, mentions the unity of <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, Persians, Bactrians and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdians" title="Sogdians">Sogdians</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Language.2C_Gherardo_Gnoli_2002_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Language.2C_Gherardo_Gnoli_2002-46">[40]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The name of <i><a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Ariana</a></i> is further extended to a part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> and of Media, as also to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bactrians" title="Bactrians">Bactrians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdians" title="Sogdians">Sogdians</a> on the north; for these speak approximately the same language, with but slight variations.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Geography, 15.8</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The trilingual inscription erected by Shapur's command gives us a more clear description. The languages used are <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">Parthian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> and Greek. In Greek the inscription says: "ego ... tou Arianon ethnous despotes eimi" which translates to "I am the king of the Aryans". In the Middle Persian Shapour says: "I am the Lord of the EranShahr" and in Parthian he says: "I am the Lord of AryanShahr".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_language" title="Bactrian language">Bactrian language</a> (a Middle Iranian language) inscription of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanishka_the_Great" title="Kanishka the Great">Kanishka the Great</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> at Rabatak, which was discovered in 1993 in an unexcavated site in the Afghanistan province of <a href="/wiki/Baghlan" title="Baghlan">Baghlan</a>, clearly refers to this Eastern Iranian language as Arya<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> In the post-Islamic era one can still see a clear usage of the term Aryan (Iran) in the work of the 10th-century historian <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hamzah_al-Isfahani&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hamzah al-Isfahani (page does not exist)">Hamzah al-Isfahani</a>. In his famous book "The History of Prophets and Kings", al-Isfahani writes, "Aryan which is also called <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Pars</a> is in the middle of these countries and these six countries surround it because the South East is in the hands China, the North of the Turks, the middle South is India, the middle North is Rome, and the South West and the North West is the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and Berber lands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup> All this evidence shows that the name arya "Iranian" was a collective definition, denoting peoples (Geiger, pp. 167 f.; Schmitt, 1978, p. 31) who were aware of belonging to the one ethnic stock, speaking a common language, and having a religious tradition that centered on the cult of Ahura Mazdā.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica.com_43-7"><a href="#cite_note-iranica.com-43">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>, the original self-identifier lives on in ethnic names like "<a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_dialect" title="Iron dialect">Iron</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-44">[38]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Iran" title="Name of Iran">The word <i>Iran</i></a> is the Persian word for land/place of the Aryan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-30">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Latin_literature">In Latin literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In Latin literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word Arianus was used to designate Ariana, the area comprising present <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> in the western part of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and ancient India. In 1601, Philemon Holland used 'Arianes' in his translation of the Latin Arianus to designate the inhabitants of Ariana. This was the first use of the form <i>Arian</i> verbatim in the English language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-59">[53]</a></sup> In 1844 James Cowles Prichard first designated both the Indians and the Iranians "Arians" under the false assumption that the Iranians as well as the Indians self-designated themselves <i>Aria</i>. The Iranians did use the form <i>Airya</i> as a designation for the "Aryans," but Prichard had mistaken <i>Aria</i> (deriving from OPer. Haravia) as a designation of the "Aryans" and associated the <i>Aria</i> with the place-name <i>Ariana</i> (Av. Airyana), the homeland of the Aryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup> The form <i>Aria</i> as a designation of the "Aryans" was, however, only preserved in the language of the Indo-Aryans.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_European_languages">In European languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In European languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "Aryan" came to be used as the term for the newly discovered <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, and, by extension, the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">original speakers of those languages</a>. In the 19th century, "language" was considered a property of "ethnicity", and thus the speakers of the Indo-Iranian or Indo-European languages came to be called the "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>", as contradistinguished from what came to be called the "<a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic race</a>". By the late 19th century, among some people, the notions of an "Aryan race" became closely linked to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nordicism" title="Nordicism">Nordicism</a>, which posited Northern European racial superiority over all other peoples. This "<a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>" ideal engendered both the "<a href="/wiki/Aryanization_(Nazism)" title="Aryanization (Nazism)">Aryanization</a>" programs of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, in which the classification of people as "Aryan" and "non-Aryan" was most emphatically directed towards the exclusion of <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[note 6]</a></sup> By the end of World War II, the word 'Aryan' had become associated by many with the racial ideologies and atrocities committed by the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazis</a>.</p>
<p>Western notions of an "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>" rose to prominence in late-19th- and early-20th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racialism_(racial_categorization)" title="Racialism (racial categorization)">racialism</a>, an idea most notably embraced by <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>. The Nazis believed that the "<a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic peoples</a>" (who were also referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>") represent an ideal and "pure race" that was the purest representation of the original racial stock of those who were then called the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Aryans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> declared that the "Nordics" were the true Aryans because they claimed that they were more "pure" (less racially mixed) than other people of what were then called the "Aryan people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hitler1925_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-hitler1925-64">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_the_19th_century">Before the 19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Before the 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the original meaning of Indo-Iranian <i>*arya</i> as a self-designator is uncontested, the origin of the word (and thus also its original meaning) remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[note 7]</a></sup> Indo-Iranian <i>ar-</i> is a syllable ambiguous in origin, from Indo-European <i>ar-</i>, <i>er-</i>, or <i>or-</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-25">[22]</a></sup> No evidence for a Proto-Indo-European (as opposed to Indo-Iranian) ethnic name like "Aryan" has been found. The word was used by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> in reference to the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> whom he describes as the people who "were once universally known as Aryans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002180_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002180-66">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The meaning of 'Aryan' that was adopted into the English language in the late 18th century was the one associated with the technical term used in comparative philology, which in turn had the same meaning as that evident in the very oldest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Indic" title="Old Indic">Old Indic</a> usage, i.e. as a (self-) identifier of "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_peoples" title="Indo-Aryan peoples">(speakers of) North Indian languages</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-59">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[note 8]</a></sup> This usage was simultaneously influenced by a word that appeared in classical sources (Latin and Greek <i>Ἀριάνης</i> <i>Arianes</i>, e.g. in Pliny 1.133 and Strabo 15.2.1–8), and recognized to be the same as that which appeared in living Iranian languages, where it was a (self-)identifier of the "<a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">(speakers of) Iranian languages</a>". Accordingly, 'Aryan' came to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">languages of the Indo-Iranian language group</a>, and by extension, native <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">speakers of those languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avestan">Avestan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Avestan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The term Arya is used in ancient Persian texts, for example in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">behistun inscription</a> from the 5th century BCE, in which the Persian kings <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes</a> are described as "Aryans of Aryan stock" (<i>arya arya chiça</i>). The inscription also refers to the deity <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> as "the god of the Aryans", and to the ancient Persian language as "Aryan". In this sense the word seems to have referred to the elite culture of the ancient Iranians, including both linguistic, cultural and religious aspects. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant2002180_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant2002180-66">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuz.27mina2007371-372_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuz.27mina2007371-372-69">[60]</a></sup> The word also has a central place in the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian religion</a> in which the "Aryan expanse" (<i>Airyana Vaejah</i>) is described as the mythical homeland of the Iranian people's and as the center of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2011_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2011-70">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedic_Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Vedic Sanskrit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The term Arya is used 36 times in 34 hymns in the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>. According to Talageri (2000, The Rig Veda. A Historical Analysis) "<i>the particular Vedic Aryans of the Rigveda were one section among these <a href="/wiki/Puru_(Vedic_tribe)" title="Puru (Vedic tribe)">Purus</a>, who called themselves <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81ratas" title="Bhāratas">Bharatas</a>.</i>" Thus it is possible, according to Talageri, that at one point Arya did refer to a specific tribe.</p>
<p>While the word may ultimately derive from a tribal name, already in the Rigveda it appears as a religious distinction, separating those who sacrifice "properly" from those who do not belong to the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">historical Vedic religion</a>, presaging the usage in later Hinduism where the term comes to denote religious righteousness or piety. In <a href="/wiki/Mandala_9" title="Mandala 9">RV 9</a>.63.5, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">ârya</span></i> "noble, pious, righteous" is used as contrasting with <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">árāvan</span></i> "not liberal, envious, hostile":</p>
<dl>
<dd><i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">índraṃ várdhanto aptúraḥ kṛṇvánto víśvam âryam apaghnánto árāvṇaḥ</span></i></dd>
<dd>"[the <a href="/wiki/Soma_(drink)" title="Soma (drink)">Soma</a>-drops], performing every noble work, active, augmenting Indra's strength, driving away the godless ones." (trans. Griffith)</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanskrit_Epics">Sanskrit Epics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sanskrit Epics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Arya and Anarya are primarily used in the moral sense in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_Epics" title="Hindu Epics">Hindu Epics</a>. People are usually called Arya or Anarya based on their behaviour. Arya is typically one who follows the Dharma.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is historically applicable for any person living anywhere in Bharata Varsha or vast India.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ramayana">Ramayana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ramayana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the Ramayana, the term Arya can also apply to Raksasas or to Ravana. In several instances, the <a href="/wiki/Vanara" title="Vanara">Vanaras</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raksasa" title="Raksasa">Raksasas</a> called themselves Arya. The vanara's king Sugriva is called an Arya (Ram: 505102712) and he also speaks of his brother Vali as an Arya (Ram: 402402434). In another instance in the Ramayana, Ravana regards himself and his ministers as Aryas (Ram: A logical explanation is that, Ravana and his ministers belonged to the highest <a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">varna</a> (Ravana being a Brahmin), and Brahmins were generally considered 'noble' of deed and hence called Arya (noble). Thus, while Ravana was considered Arya (and regarded himself as such), he was not really an Arya because he was not noble of deeds. So, he is widely considered by Hindus as Anarya (non-Arya).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a> describes <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a> as: <i>arya sarva samascaiva sadaiva priyadarsanah</i>, meaning "Arya, who worked for the equality of all and was dear to everyone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mahabharata">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a>, the terms Arya or Anarya are often applied to people according to their behaviour. <a href="/wiki/Dushasana" title="Dushasana">Dushasana</a>, who tried to disrobe <a href="/wiki/Draupadi" title="Draupadi">Draupadi</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kaurava" title="Kaurava">Kaurava</a> court, is called an "Anarya" (Mbh:0020600253). <a href="/wiki/Vidura" title="Vidura">Vidura</a>, the son of a Dasi born from <a href="/wiki/Vyasa" title="Vyasa">Vyasa</a>, was the only person in the assembly whose behaviour is called "Arya", because he was the only one who openly protested when Draupadi was being disrobed by Dushasana. The <a href="/wiki/Pandava" title="Pandava">Pandavas</a> called themselves "Arya" in the Mahabharata (0071670471) when they killed <a href="/wiki/Drona" title="Drona">Drona</a> through deception.</p>
<p>According to the Mahabharata, a person's behaviour (not wealth or learning) determines if he can be called an Arya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronkhorst_1999_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bronkhorst_1999-73">[64]</a></sup> Also the whole Kuru clan was called as Anarya .</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_use">Religious use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religious use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The word <i>ārya</i> is often found in Hindu, Buddhist, and Jain texts. In the Indian spiritual context it can be applied to Rishis or to someone who has mastered the four noble truths and entered upon the spiritual path. According to <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Nehru</a>, the religions of India may be called collectively <i>ārya dharma,</i> a term that includes the religions that originated in India (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>"<i>O my Lord, a person who is chanting Your holy name, although born of a low family like that of a <a href="/wiki/Chandala" title="Chandala">Chandala</a>, is situated on the highest platform of self-realization. Such a person must have performed all kinds of penances and sacrifices according to Vedic literatures many, many times after taking bath in all the holy places of pilgrimage. Such a person is considered to be the best of the Arya family</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a> 3.33.7).</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>, "<i>A child materially born is not an Arya; the child born in spirituality is an Arya.</i>" He further elaborated, referring to the <a href="/wiki/Manu_Smriti" title="Manu Smriti">Manu Smriti</a>: "<i>Says our great law-giver, <a href="/wiki/Manu_(Hinduism)" title="Manu (Hinduism)">Manu</a>, giving the definition of an Arya, 'He is the Arya, who is born through prayer.' Every child not born through prayer is illegitimate, according to the great law-giver: "The child must be prayed for. Those children that come with curses, that slip into the world, just in a moment of inadvertence, because that could not be prevented – what can we expect of such progeny?...</i>"(Swami Vivekananda, Complete Works vol.8)</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dayananda_Saraswati" title="Dayananda Saraswati">Swami Dayananda</a> founded a Dharmic organisation <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a> in 1875. <a href="/wiki/Sri_Aurobindo" title="Sri Aurobindo">Sri Aurobindo</a> published a journal combining nationalism and spiritualism under the title <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arya_(journal)" title="Arya (journal)">Arya</a></i> from 1914 to 1921.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arya_(Buddhism)" title="Arya (Buddhism)">Arya (Buddhism)</a></div>
<p>The word <i>ārya</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" title="Pāli">Pāli</a>: <i>ariya</i>), in the sense "noble" or "exalted", is very frequently used in Buddhist texts to designate a spiritual warrior or hero, which use this term much more often than Hindu or Jain texts. Buddha's <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">Vinaya</a> are the <i>ariyassa dhammavinayo</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> are called the <i>catvāry āryasatyāni</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>) or <i>cattāri ariyasaccāni</i> (Pali). The <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a> is called the <i>āryamārga</i> (Sanskrit, also <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">āryāṣṭāṅgikamārga</span></i>) or <i>ariyamagga</i> (Pāli). Buddhists themselves are called <i>ariyapuggalas</i> (Arya persons). In Buddhist texts, the āryas are those who have the Buddhist <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%ABla" title="Śīla">śīla</a></i> (Pāli <i>sīla</i>, meaning "virtue") and follow the Buddhist path. Those who despise Buddhism are often called "anāryas".</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Jainism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The word Arya is also often used in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, in Jain texts such as the Pannavanasutta.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 19th century, linguists still supposed that the age of a language determined its "superiority" (because it was assumed to have genealogical purity). Then, based on the assumption that Sanskrit was the oldest Indo-European language, and the (now known to be untenable)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Language_History.2C_Language_Change.2C_and_Language_Relationship_by_Hans_Henrich_Hock.2C_Brian_D._Joseph_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Language_History.2C_Language_Change.2C_and_Language_Relationship_by_Hans_Henrich_Hock.2C_Brian_D._Joseph-75">[66]</a></sup> position that Irish <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ire" title="Éire">Éire</a> was etymologically related to "Aryan", in 1837 <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Pictet" title="Adolphe Pictet">Adolphe Pictet</a> popularized the idea that the term "Aryan" could also be applied to the entire Indo-European language family as well. The groundwork for this thought had been laid by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Hyacinthe_Anquetil-Duperron" title="Abraham Hyacinthe Anquetil-Duperron">Abraham Hyacinthe Anquetil-Duperron</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In particular, German scholar <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Schlegel" title="Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel">Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel</a> published in 1819 the first theory linking the Indo-Iranian and the German languages under the Aryan group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHD_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHD-38">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[68]</a></sup> In 1830 <a href="/wiki/Karl_Otfried_M%C3%BCller" title="Karl Otfried Müller">Karl Otfried Müller</a> used "Arier" in his publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following this linguistic argument, in the 1850s <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a> supposed that "Aryan" corresponded to the suggested prehistoric Indo-European culture (1853–1855, <i>Essay on the Inequality of the Human Races</i>). Further, de Gobineau believed that there were three basic races – white, yellow and black – and that everything else was caused by race <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a>, which de Gobineau argued was the cause of chaos. The "<a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>", according to de Gobineau, were the Northern European "Aryans", who had remained "racially pure". Southern Europeans (to include Spaniards and Southern Frenchmen), Eastern Europeans, North Africans, Middle Easterners, Iranians, Central Asians, Indians, he all considered racially mixed, degenerated through the <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a>, and thus less than ideal.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="464" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg/275px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg/413px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The earliest epigraphically attested reference to the word <i>ariya</i> occurs in the 6th century BCE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">Behistun inscription</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kermanshah_Province" title="Kermanshah Province">Kermanshah</a>, which describes itself to have been composed "in <i>ariya</i> [language or script]" (¶ 70). As is also the case for all other Old Iranian language usage, the <i>ariya</i> of the inscription does not signify anything but "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Iran" title="Etymology of Iran">Iranian</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gershevitch_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gershevitch-15">[15]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the 1880s a number of linguists and anthropologists argued that the "Aryans" themselves had originated somewhere in northern Europe. A specific region began to crystallize when the linguist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Penka" title="Karl Penka">Karl Penka</a> (<i>Die Herkunft der Arier. Neue Beiträge zur historischen Anthropologie der europäischen Völker</i>, 1886) popularized the idea that the "Aryans" had emerged in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and could be identified by the distinctive Nordic characteristics of blond hair and blue eyes. The distinguished biologist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a> agreed with him, coining the term "Xanthochroi" to refer to fair-skinned Europeans (as opposed to darker Mediterranean peoples, who Huxley called "Melanochroi").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1533" data-file-width="2192" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg/300px-Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg/450px-Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg/600px-Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Great_Race_-_Map_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Madison_Grant" title="Madison Grant">Madison Grant</a>'s vision of the distribution of "Nordics" (red), "Alpines" (green) and "Mediterraneans" (yellow).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="957" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png/300px-Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png/450px-Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png/600px-Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ripley_map_of_cephalic_index_in_Europe.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/William_Z._Ripley" title="William Z. Ripley">William Z. Ripley</a>'s map of the "cephalic index" in Europe, from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Races_of_Europe_(1899_book)" title="The Races of Europe (1899 book)">The Races of Europe</a></i> (1899).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This "<a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic race</a>" theory gained traction following the publication of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Morris_(American_writer)" title="Charles Morris (American writer)">Charles Morris</a>'s <i>The Aryan Race</i> (1888), which touches racist ideology. A similar rationale was followed by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Vacher_de_Lapouge" title="Georges Vacher de Lapouge">Georges Vacher de Lapouge</a> in his book <i>L'Aryen et son rôle social</i> (1899, "The Aryan and his Social Role"). To this idea of "races", Vacher de Lapouge espoused what he termed <i><a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">selectionism</a></i>, and which had two aims: first, achieving the annihilation of trade unionists, considered "degenerate"; second, the prevention of labour dissatisfaction through the creation of "types" of man, each "designed" for one specific task (See the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i> for a fictional treatment of this idea).</p>
<p>Meanwhile, in India, the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British colonial government</a> had followed de Gobineau's arguments along another line, and had fostered the idea of a superior "Aryan race" that co-opted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_caste_system" title="Indian caste system">Indian caste system</a> in favor of imperial interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thapar_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thapar-80">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leopold_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leopold-81">[72]</a></sup> In its fully developed form, the British-mediated interpretation foresaw a segregation of Aryan and non-Aryan along the lines of caste, with the upper castes being "Aryan" and the lower ones being "non-Aryan". The European developments not only allowed the British to identify themselves as high-caste, but also allowed the Brahmans to view themselves as on-par with the British. Further, it provoked the reinterpretation of Indian history in racialist and, in opposition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Nationalist" title="Indian Nationalist">Indian Nationalist</a> terms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thapar_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thapar-80">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leopold_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leopold-81">[72]</a></sup> and – in following a special interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>'s identification of "Aryan" as a national name – this gave rise recently among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_nationalists" title="Hindu nationalists">Hindu nationalists</a> (the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffron_Brigade" title="Saffron Brigade">Saffron Brigade</a>") to the "<a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Aryans" title="Indigenous Aryans">indigenous Aryans</a>" or so-called "Out of India" theory, disputed by many scholars in academia, which seeks an Indian origin of the Indo-European "Aryans".</p>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Doctrine" title="The Secret Doctrine">The Secret Doctrine</a></i> (1888), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky" title="Helena Petrovna Blavatsky">Helena Petrovna Blavatsky</a> described the "<a href="/wiki/Root_race#The_fifth_root_race_.28Aryan.29" title="Root race">Aryan root race</a>" as the fifth of seven "<a href="/wiki/Root_race" title="Root race">Root races</a>", dating their <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">souls</a> as having begun to <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">incarnate</a> about a million years ago in <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a>. The Semites were a subdivision of the Aryan root race. "The occult doctrine admits of no such divisions as the Aryan and the Semite, ... The Semites, especially the Arabs, are later Aryans — degenerate in spirituality and perfected in materiality. To these belong all the Jews and the Arabs." The Jews, according to Blavatsky, were a "tribe descended from the <a href="/wiki/Chandala" title="Chandala">Tchandalas</a> of India," as they were born of Abraham, which she believed to be a corruption of a word meaning "No <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmin</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[73]</a></sup> Other sources suggest the origin Avram or Aavram.</p>
<p>The name for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanian_Empire" title="Sassanian Empire">Sassanian Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> is <i>Eran Shahr</i> which means <i>Aryan Empire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[74]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Iran" title="Islamic conquest of Iran">Islamic conquest</a> in Iran, racialist rhetoric became a literary idiom during the 7th century, i.e., when the Arabs became the primary "<a href="/wiki/Other" title="Other">Other</a>" – the <a href="/wiki/Aniran" title="Aniran"><i>anarya</i>s</a> – and the antithesis of everything Iranian (i.e. Aryan) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a>. But "the antecedents of [present-day] Iranian ultra-nationalism can be traced back to the writings of late nineteenth-century figures such as <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Fatali_Akhundov" title="Mirza Fatali Akhundov">Mirza Fatali Akhundov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Aqa_Khan_Kermani" title="Mirza Aqa Khan Kermani">Mirza Aqa Khan Kermani</a>. Demonstrating affinity with Orientalist views of the supremacy of the <i><a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan peoples</a></i> and the mediocrity of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic_peoples" title="Semitic peoples">Semitic peoples</a></i>, Iranian nationalist discourse idealized pre-Islamic [<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a>] empires, whilst negating the 'Islamization' of <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persia</a> by Muslim forces."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MRZ_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MRZ-84">[75]</a></sup> In the 20th century, different aspects of this idealization of a distant past would be instrumentalized by both the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_dynasty" title="Pahlavi dynasty">Pahlavi monarchy</a> (In 1967, Iran's <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_dynasty" title="Pahlavi dynasty">Pahlavi</a> <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasty</a> [overthrown in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1979_Iranian_Revolution" title="1979 Iranian Revolution">1979 Iranian Revolution</a>] added the title <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aryamehr" title="Aryamehr">Āryāmehr</a> <i>Light of the Aryans</i> to the other styles of the <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Iranian monarch</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shah_of_Iran" title="Shah of Iran">Shah of Iran</a> being already known at that time as the <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Shahanshah</a> (<i>King of Kings</i>)), and by the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Islamic republic</a> that followed it; the Pahlavis used it as a foundation for anticlerical monarchism, and the clerics used it to exalt Iranian values vis-á-vis westernization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddie_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keddie-85">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="480" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg/275px-Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg/413px-Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birth_of_a_nation_Aryan_quote.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Intertitle" title="Intertitle">intertitle</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> blockbuster <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">The Birth of a Nation</a></i> (1915). "Aryan birthright" is here "white birthright", the "defense" of which unites "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_race" title="White race">whites</a>" in the Northern and Southern U.S. against "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coloreds" title="Coloreds">coloreds</a>". In another film of the same year, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Aryan" title="The Aryan">The Aryan</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/William_S._Hart" title="William S. Hart">William S. Hart</a>'s "Aryan" identity is defined in distinction from <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Americans" title="Mexican Americans">Mexicans</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the United States, the best-selling 1907 book <i><a href="/wiki/Race_Life_of_the_Aryan_Peoples" title="Race Life of the Aryan Peoples">Race Life of the Aryan Peoples</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Pomeroy_Widney" title="Joseph Pomeroy Widney">Joseph Pomeroy Widney</a> consolidated in the popular mind the idea that the word "Aryan" is the proper identification for "all Indo-Europeans", and that "<a href="/wiki/European_Americans" title="European Americans">Aryan Americans</a>" of the "Aryan race" are destined to fulfill America's <a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">manifest destiny</a> to form an <a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">American Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon Childe would later regret it, but the depiction of Aryans as possessors of a "superior language" became a matter of national pride in learned circles of Germany (portrayed against the background that World War I was lost because Germany had been betrayed from within by miscegenation and the "corruption" of socialist trade unionists and other "degenerates").</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a>—one of the principal architects of Nazi ideological creed—argued for a new "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_and_soil" title="Blood and soil">religion of the blood</a>", based on the supposed innate promptings of the Nordic soul to defend its "noble" character against racial and cultural degeneration. Under Rosenberg, the theories of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Vacher_de_Lapouge" title="Georges Vacher de Lapouge">Georges Vacher de Lapouge</a>, Blavatsky, <a href="/wiki/Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain" title="Houston Stewart Chamberlain">Houston Stewart Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madison_Grant" title="Madison Grant">Madison Grant</a>, and those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitler" title="Hitler">Hitler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[78]</a></sup> all culminated in <a href="/wiki/Racial_policy_of_Nazi_Germany" title="Racial policy of Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany's race policies</a> and the "<a href="/wiki/Aryanization_(Nazism)" title="Aryanization (Nazism)">Aryanization</a>" decrees of the 1920s, 1930s, and early 1940s. In its "apalling medical model", the annihilation of the "racially inferior" <i><a href="/wiki/Untermensch" title="Untermensch">Untermenschen</a></i> was sanctified as the excision of a diseased organ in an otherwise healthy body,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[79]</a></sup> which led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
