<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Vedic_India.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="683" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Map_of_Vedic_India.png/250px-Map_of_Vedic_India.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Map_of_Vedic_India.png/375px-Map_of_Vedic_India.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Map_of_Vedic_India.png/500px-Map_of_Vedic_India.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Vedic_India.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In academic scholarship, the only surviving use of the word "Aryan" among many scholars is that of the term "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a>", which indicates "(speakers of) languages descended from <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrits</a>". Older usage to mean "(speakers of) <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a>" has been superseded among some scholars by the term "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranian</a>"; however, "Aryan" is still used to mean "Indo-Iranian" by other scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Josef_Wiesehofer" title="Josef Wiesehofer">Josef Wiesehofer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Luca_Cavalli-Sforza" title="Luigi Luca Cavalli-Sforza">Luigi Luca Cavalli-Sforza</a>. The 19th century meaning of "Aryan" as (native speakers of) <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>" is no longer used by most scholars, but has continued among some scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colin_Renfrew" title="Colin Renfrew">Colin Renfrew</a>, and among some authors writing for the popular mass market such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.G._Wells" title="H.G. Wells">H.G. Wells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poul_Anderson" title="Poul Anderson">Poul Anderson</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the end of World War II, the word "Aryan" among a number of people had lost its Romantic or idealist connotations and was associated by many with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_racism" title="Nazi racism">Nazi racism</a> instead.</p>
<p>By then, the term "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranian</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European" title="Indo-European">Indo-European</a>" had made most uses of the term "Aryan" superfluous in the eyes of a number of scholars, and "Aryan" now survives in most scholarly usage only in the term "<a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a>" to indicate (speakers of) North Indian languages. It has been asserted by one scholar that Indo-Aryan and Aryan may not be equated and that such an equation is not supported by the historical evidence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1991_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1991-89">[80]</a></sup> though this extreme viewpoint is not widespread.</p>
<p>The use of the term to designate speakers of all Indo-European languages in scholarly usage is now regarded by some scholars as an "aberration to be avoided."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[81]</a></sup> However, some authors writing for popular consumption have continued using the word "Aryan" for "all Indo-Europeans" in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[83]</a></sup> such as the science fiction author <a href="/wiki/Poul_Anderson" title="Poul Anderson">Poul Anderson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[84]</a></sup> and scientists writing for the popular media, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colin_Renfrew" title="Colin Renfrew">Colin Renfrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[85]</a></sup> Notions of the "Aryan race" as an elite group that is regarded as being superior to other races survive in some <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> European groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazism" title="Neo-Nazism">Neo-Nazi</a> parties, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_nationalism_in_Russia" title="Radical nationalism in Russia">Russian ultra-nationalists</a>, as well as in certain <a href="/wiki/Iranian_nationalism" title="Iranian nationalism">Iranian nationalist</a> groups.</p>
<p>Echoes of "the 19th century prejudice about 'northern' Aryans who were confronted on Indian soil with black barbarians [...] can still be heard in some modern studies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1991_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1991-89">[80]</a></sup> In a socio-political context, the claim of a white, European Aryan race that includes only people of the Western and not the Eastern branch of the Indo-European peoples is entertained by certain circles, usually representing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_nationalist" title="White nationalist">white nationalists</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[86]</a></sup> who call for the halting of non-white immigration into Europe and limiting <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration" title="Illegal immigration">illegal immigration</a> into the United States. They argue that a large intrusion of immigrants can lead to <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_conflict" title="Ethnic conflict">ethnic conflicts</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/2005_Cronulla_riots" title="2005 Cronulla riots">2005 Cronulla riots</a> in Australia and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2005_civil_unrest_in_France" title="2005 civil unrest in France">2005 civil unrest in France</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aryan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fortson, IV: "The Sanskrit word <i>ārya-</i>, the source of the English word, was the self-designation of the Vedic Indic people and has a cognate in Iranian <i>*arya</i>, where it is also a self-desigmation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">J. P. Mallory and Douglas Q. Adams: "Our ability to reconstruct a Proto Indo-Iranian intermediate between Proto-Indo-European on the one hand and Proto-Indic and Proto-Iranian is also supported by the self-designation, *aryo-."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997304-21">[20]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Both the Indic and Iranian terms descend from a form <i>*ārya</i> that was used by the Indo-Iranian tribes to refer to themselves. (It is also the source of the country-name <i>Iran</i>, from a phrase meaning 'kingdom of the Aryans'.)"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson.2C_IV2011209-8">[8]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Avestan airya may also be connected with Indo-Iranian *ara-.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuchesne-Guillemin1979337_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuchesne-Guillemin1979337-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[a]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>American Heritage Dictionary of the English Language</i> states at the beginning of its definition, "[it] is one of the ironies of history that <i>Aryan</i>, a word nowadays referring to the blond-haired, blue-eyed physical ideal of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, originally referred to a people who looked vastly different. Its history starts with the ancient <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranians</a>, peoples who inhabited parts of what are now <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, Pakistan and India. "<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHD_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHD-38">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Under the 1933 <i>Law for the Restoration of the Professional Civil Service</i>, a non-Aryan was defined as "an individual descended from a non-Aryan (in particular Jewish parents or grandparents)" (<a href="#CITEREFCampt2004">Campt 2004</a>, p. 143).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is no shortage of ideas, even in the present-day. For a summary of the etymological problems involved, see <a href="#CITEREFSiegert1941.E2.80.931942">Siegert 1941–1942</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The context being religious, Max Müller understood this to especially mean "the worshipers of the gods of the Brahmans". If this is seen from the point of view of the religious poets of the RigVedic hymns, an 'Aryan' was then a person who held the same religious convictions as the poet himself. This idea can then also be found in Iranian texts.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991125_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMallory1991">Mallory 1991</a>, p. 125.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Oxford_English_Dictionary-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Dictionary_5-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://dictionary.oed.com/cgi/entry/50012669?single=1&amp;query_type=word&amp;queryword=aryan&amp;first=1&amp;max_to_show=10" rel="nofollow">Oxford English Dictionary: "Aryan from Sanskrit Arya 'Noble'"</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-AHD-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AHD_38-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AHD_38-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFWatkins2000">Watkins, Calvert (2000), "Aryan", <i>American Heritage Dictionary of the English Language</i> (4th ed.), New York: Houghton Mifflin, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-395-82517-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-395-82517-2">0-395-82517-2</a>, <q>...when <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Schlegel" title="Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel">Friedrich Schlegel</a>, a German scholar who was an important early <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-Europeanist</a>, came up with a theory that linked the Indo-Iranian words with the German word <i>Ehre</i>, 'honor', and older Germanic names containing the element <i>ario-</i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Swiss</a>  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] warrior <a href="/wiki/Ariovistus" title="Ariovistus">Ariovistus</a> who was written about by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>. Schlegel theorized that far from being just a designation of the Indo-Iranians, the word <i>*arya-</i> had in fact been what the Indo-Europeans called themselves, meaning [according to Schlegel] something like 'the honorable people.' (This theory has since been called into question.)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAryan&amp;rft.atitle=Aryan&amp;rft.aufirst=Calvert&amp;rft.aulast=Watkins&amp;rft.btitle=American+Heritage+Dictionary+of+the+English+Language&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.edition=4th&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=0-395-82517-2&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Houghton+Mifflin&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Schmitt-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmitt_44-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">R. Schmitt, "Aryans" in Encyclopædia Iranica: Excerpt:"The name "Aryan" (OInd. āˊrya-, Ir. *arya- [with short a-], in Old Pers. ariya-, Av. airiia-, etc.) is the self designation of the peoples of Ancient India and Ancient Iran who spoke Aryan languages, in contrast to the "non-Aryan" peoples of those "Aryan" countries (cf. OInd. an-āˊrya-, Av. an-airiia-, etc.), and lives on in ethnic names like Alan (Lat. Alani, NPers. Īrān, Oss. Ir and Iron). Also accessed online: <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/aryans" rel="nofollow">[8]</a> in May 2010</span></li>

Excerpt: "ARYA an ethnic epithet in the Achaemenid inscriptions and in the Zoroastrian Avestan tradition. <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/arya-an-ethnic-epithet-in-the-achaemenid-inscriptions-and-in-the-zoroastrian-avestan-tradition" rel="nofollow">[9]</a> Also accessed online in May, 2010.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_50-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">R.G. <li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Professor <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Lazard" title="Gilbert Lazard">Gilbert Lazard</a>: "The language known as New Persian, which usually is called at this period (early Islamic times) by the name of Dari or Parsi-Dari, can be classified linguistically as a continuation of Middle Persian, the official religious and literary language of Sassanian Iran, itself a continuation of Old Persian, the language of the Achaemenids. Unlike the other languages and dialects, ancient and modern, of the Iranian group such as Avestan, Parthian, Soghdian, Kurdish, Balochi, Pashto, etc., Old Middle and New Persian represent one and the same language at three states of its history. It had its origin in Fars (the true Persian country from the historical point of view) and is differentiated by dialectical features, still easily recognizable from the dialect prevailing in north-western and eastern Iran" in Lazard, Gilbert 1975, "The Rise of the New Persian Language" in Frye, R. N., <i>The Cambridge History of Iran</i>, Vol. 4, pp. 595–632, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press.</span></li>
